Website Ai Designer said:
âœ… You're absolutely right to ask this â€” itâ€™s one of the first things every professional web developer eventually runs into:

â€œDo I seriously have to copy/paste the same nav and footer across every page?â€

And the answer is:

âŒ No â€” you shouldnâ€™t have to.
âœ… Yes â€” there are ways to fix that.

âœ… The Figma Equivalent for Web: Reusable Components
In Figma, components let you change something once and have it update everywhere. In web development, we achieve this with:

ğŸ” Reusable partials â€” like shared header/footer includes.
ğŸ§  So Why Are We Copy/Pasting Right Now?
Youâ€™re working with static HTML files (no Node, PHP, or React rendering engine), so:

Browsers do not natively support includes or components in .html files

Thatâ€™s why you're manually duplicating the nav/footer block in each page

It's simple and portable â€” but not scalable

âœ… Your MVP Options (Ranked by Simplicity)
âœ… Option 1: Use JavaScript Includes (Best for your project)
We can load a shared header.html and footer.html into every page using JavaScript:

<!-- index.html -->
<body>
  <div id="site-header"></div>

  <!-- Page content -->

  <div id="site-footer"></div>
</body>

<script>
  fetch('/partials/header.html')
    .then(res => res.text())
    .then(html => document.getElementById('site-header').innerHTML = html);

  fetch('/partials/footer.html')
    .then(res => res.text())
    .then(html => document.getElementById('site-footer').innerHTML = html);
</script>
Then you can move your nav bar and footer into:

/partials/header.html

/partials/footer.html

Now, you can change your nav/footer once, and every page stays in sync.
Itâ€™s basically Figma components, but for your static HTML site.

âœ… Benefits:
Works with pure static sites (like yours)

No framework or server required

Easy to update later

âš ï¸ Caveats:
Requires JavaScript (wonâ€™t work if JS is disabled)

May not render in some older web crawlers or offline tools

âœ… Option 2: Use a Build Tool (Later)
Later, once you're out of MVP mode, you could use:

11ty (Eleventy) â€“ static site generator

Jekyll â€“ supported on GitHub Pages

Astro â€“ modern static framework

[Parcel, Vite, or Webpack] with templating

These allow you to write one nav/footer and have them injected into every page at build time â€” just like reusable components in Figma.

But for now? Stick with Option 1.

âœ… You're absolutely right â€” and I know exactly why this happened.

ğŸ¯ What Happened?
When I rewrote the custom.css from scratch, I did include this line:

  position: sticky;
  top: 0;
  z-index: 1000;
  background-color: var(--jukebox-red);
  border-bottom: 2px solid var(--jukebox-gold);
}
âœ… That should have made the header sticky...

BUT â€” in order for position: sticky; to work reliably in CSS:

â— You must ensure:
The parent of the sticky element is not overflow: hidden or overflow: auto

The sticky element is inside the body (not dynamically loaded into a container that breaks scroll context)

The sticky element actually exists at the time the page renders

ğŸ§  Why Your Header Isnâ€™t Sticky (Now)
You're dynamically injecting the header using:

fetch('/partials/header.html')
  .then(res => res.text())
  .then(html => document.getElementById('site-header').innerHTML = html);
This loads after the initial page render, and some CSS behavior like sticky positioning can break when added this way â€” depending on how the browser calculates layout.

âœ… Simple Fixes You Can Apply Right Now
âœ… Option 1: Apply position: sticky directly in the partial
Open your partials/header.html file and wrap the nav in a div with class sticky-header, like this:

<!-- partials/header.html -->
<div class="sticky-header">
  <nav class="navbar navbar-expand-lg">
    <!-- your nav content -->
  </nav>
</div>
Then, in custom.css, add this rule to ensure stickiness works independently of how it's loaded:

.sticky-header {
  position: sticky;
  top: 0;
  z-index: 1000;
}
This ensures that even if the nav bar is injected late, the outer wrapper behaves as a sticky element.

âœ… Option 2: Move .sticky-header to index.html (Not Preferred)
You could wrap the injected #site-header in a div that's already sticky before JS loads it â€” but this is messier and breaks modularity.

Stick with Option 1.

âœ… TL;DR
Problem	Fix
Sticky header no longer works	Add a div.sticky-header wrapper inside partials/header.html and apply position: sticky in CSS
Why?	Dynamically injected elements may not inherit sticky reliably unless their container is set up properly before load

âœ… What This Does Correctly:
Property	Effect

top: 0	Anchors the sticky position to the top
z-index: 1000	Ensures it layers above other content
background-color	Sets red background to match your theme

âœ… So yes â€” this is technically correct sticky header code.

âŒ Why It Might Still Fail in Practice

1. The <nav> is being loaded dynamically (via JS/partial)
Sticky behavior sometimes breaks if the layout isnâ€™t fully painted before itâ€™s inserted

2. The .navbar is not a direct child of <body>
If it's inside a div that has overflow: hidden, or is nested in an odd way, sticky positioning fails silently

âœ… Thatâ€™s Why I Proposed This:

  top: 0;
  z-index: 1000;
}
âœ… This guarantees that even if your navbar is loaded later, or the browser miscalculates its layout parent, the wrapper remains sticky.

It also separates layout behavior (sticky) from visual style (navbar), which is cleaner and safer long-term.

Website Ai Designer said:
âœ… You're asking exactly the right set of questions â€” and answering them will set the tone for all future partial management.

Letâ€™s take them one by one:

â“ Question 1:
Should we include a <head> section with <meta name="sitemap" content="false"> or something similar to exclude partials from the sitemap?

âœ… Answer:
No. You donâ€™t need a <head> or sitemap metadata in a partial file.

The tree.json generation (your sitemap system) only scrapes full .html pages, not fragment files like /partials/header.html

The generator will ignore files that donâ€™t contain full-page structure, including <!DOCTYPE html> and <head>

So thereâ€™s no need to explicitly exclude them â€” they're invisible to the sitemap tool by design.

âœ… Conclusion: No header or meta needed in a partial file â€” correct behavior, no action required.

â“ Question 2:
Why is there no <head> in header.html? Is that just because it's a partial?

âœ… Answer:
Yes, exactly. Partials are HTML fragments, not full documents.

They should not contain: <!DOCTYPE html>, <html>, <head>, <body>

They should only contain the reusable piece: e.g., your <nav> block

So:

No <head> â€” âœ… correct

No need for <title>, <meta>, etc. â€” âœ… correct

Only layout and content structure â€” âœ… expected

â“ Question 3:

âœ… Answer:
Only if that component is reused across multiple pages.

In your case:

You do not reuse .hero-section across pages (each page has its own)

Therefore, that kind of header belongs in each page, not in the nav partial

âœ… Keep partials focused on the nav or footer only. Hero banners, page titles, etc. stay in the page file.
